تلخيص قواعد رسم المفتي
أصول الإفتاء لا بد فيه من بلوغ حد الوسع، وهو الاجتهاد. فهذا مما يفتقر إليه الحاكم في كل شاهد، كما إذا أوصى بماله للفقراء؛ فلا شك أن من الناس من لا شيء له، فيتحقق فيه اسم الفقر، فهو من أهل الوصيّة، ومنهم من لا حاجة به ولا فقر، وإن لم يملك نصاباً، وبينهما وسائط... فينظر فيه هل الغالب عليه حكم الفقر أو حكم الغنى؟ وكذلك فى فَرْض نفقات الزوجات والقرابات، إذ هو مفتقر إلى النظر في حال المنفق عليه والمنفق، وحال الوقت، إلى غير ذلك من الأمور التي لا تنضبط بحصر، ولا يمكن استيفاء القول في آحادها، فلا يمكن أن يُستغنى ههنا بالتقليد، لأن التقليد إنما يتصوّر بعد تحقيق مناط الحكم المقلد فيه والمناط هنا لم يتحقق بعد، لأن كل صورة من صوره النازلة نازلة مستأنفة في نفسها لم يتقدم لها نظير، وإن تقدم لها في نفس الأمر فلم يتقدم لنا، فلا بد من النظر فيها بالاجتهاد، وكذلك إن فرضنا أنه تقدم لنا مثلها، فلا بد من النظر في كونها مثلها أو لا، وهو نظر اجتهادي أيضاً ... ويكفيك من ذلك أنّ الشريعة لم تنص على حكم كل جزئية على حدتها، وإنما أتت بأمورٍ كُليّةٍ، وعبارات مطلقة تتناول أعداداً لا تنحصر، ومع ذلك، فلكل معيّن خصوصية ليست في غيره، ولو فى نفس التّعيين، وليس ما به الامتياز معتبراً فى الحكم بإطلاق ولا هو طردي بإطلاق بل ذلك منقسم إلى الضربين، وبينهما قسم ثالث يأخذ بجهة من الطرفين؛ فلا يبقى صورة من الصور الوجودية المعيّنة إلا وللعالم فيها نظر سهل أو صعب حتى يحقق تحت أي دليل تدخل، فإن أخذت بشبه من الطرفين، فالأمر أصعب، وهذا كله بين لمن شدا في العلم... فالحاصل أنه لا بد منه بالنسبة إلى كل ناظر وحاكم ومفت، بل بالنسبة إلى كلّ مكلّف في نفسه... ولو فرض ارتفاع هذا الاجتهاد،?
Principles of Issuing Fatwas – Summary of the Rules of the Mufti’s Manual. The actual matter asked about, and applying the ruling to it. This type of ijtihad continues until the Day of Judgment. Imam al-Shatibi, may Allah have mercy on him, elaborated on this type. So we present his words here verbatim for the benefits they contain. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'Ijtihad is of two types: One of them: it cannot cease until the origin of obligation ceases, and that is at the establishment of the Hour. The second: it can cease before the end of the world. As for the first: it is ijtihad related to tahqiq al-manat (determination of the locus of application). This is the one about which there is no disagreement among the Umma in accepting it. Its meaning is that the ruling is established by its Sharia source, but there remains the consideration of specifying its place. For example, when the Lawgiver says, “And bring to witness two witnesses of justice among you” (Talaq 2), and we have established the meaning of justice legally, we need to specify who possesses this attribute. People are not alike in the attribute of justice; rather, it varies distinctively. For if we examine the just, we find their possession of it has two extremes and a middle… and this middle ground is obscure.' (1) Imam al-Shatibi: He is Ibrahim ibn Musa ibn Muhammad al-Lakhmi al-Gharnati, Abu Ishaq, known as al-Shatibi al-Maliki, the Imam, the scholar, the hadith expert, the jurist, the usuli, the linguist, one of the renewers of his era in Andalusia. He was among the foremost investigating scholars; his works reached the peak of investigation and creativity. Among them are al-Muwafaqat, which gained widespread fame and became one of the most reliable references on the subject of Maqasid al-Sharia; and al-I’tisam, in which he refuted the innovations that appeared in his time, fearing no one in Allah, nor the blame of critics or aggression of aggressors. He died, may Allah have mercy on him, in the year 790 AH. Benefit: Note that the author of this biography is Imam Abu Ishaq al-Shatibi, and Imam al-Shatibi the author of the Shatibiyya poem on qira’at is much older than him; he is Imam Abu Muhammad al-Qasim ibn Firah al-Shatibi al-Darir, who died in Cairo in the year 590 AH. May Allah have mercy on them both. (Summary from the introductions of the editions of al-Muwafaqat and al-I’tisam; also refer to Kashf al-Zunun under ‘Harz al-Amani’ 1:646). (2) Shaykh Abdullah Darraz said in his commentary on al-Muwafaqat: 'In al-Minhaj, tahqiq al-manat is the verification of the agreed-upon ‘illa (effective cause) in the branch, i.e., establishing the evidence for its existence in it, as when two agree that the ‘illa in riba is edibility, then they differ on its existence in figs, so that it becomes subject to riba etc.… It does not fall under what is called qiyas; rather it is merely the application of the universal to its particulars.' [PAGE_160] 163 – Summary of the Rules of the Mufti’s Manual. Principles of Issuing Fatwas: It necessarily requires reaching the level of capacity (wus‘), which is ijtihad. This is something that the judge needs in every witness, as when someone bequeaths his wealth to the poor; there is no doubt that among people there are those who have nothing, so the name ‘poor’ applies to them, and they are entitled to the bequest; and among them are those who have no need and are not poor, even if they do not possess a nisab; and between them there are intermediate cases… so one considers whether the ruling of poverty or wealth dominates. Likewise, in determining maintenance for wives and relatives, it requires considering the situation of the dependents and the provider, and the situation of the time, and other matters that cannot be fully enumerated nor can exhaustive discussion of each individual one be given. Therefore, one cannot rely on taqlid (blind imitation) here, because taqlid is only conceivable after the determination of the locus of the ruling that is being followed, and the locus here has not yet been determined. Because each new incident is an independent new case that has no precedent; even if it did have a precedent in reality, it has not reached us. So there must be ijtihad in considering it. Similarly, if we assume that a similar case has reached us, we must still consider whether it is truly similar or not, and that consideration is also ijtihad… It suffices you that the Sharia did not specify the ruling for each individual case separately; rather, it brought universal principles and absolute statements that encompass countless numbers. Nevertheless, every specific entity has a particularity not found in others, even in its very specification. And the distinguishing factor is neither absolutely considered in the ruling nor absolutely irrelevant; rather, it is divided into two categories, and between them is a third category that partakes of both sides. So no individual existent case remains without the scholar having a view, easy or difficult, to determine under which evidence it falls. If it partakes of both sides, the matter is more difficult. All of this is clear to one who has advanced in knowledge. So the conclusion is that it is necessary for every observer, judge, and mufti, indeed for every responsible person regarding himself… And if it were assumed that this ijtihad were to cease,?
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage discusses the classification of ijtihad into two types by Imam al-Shatibi, focusing on the first type, which is tahqiq al-manat (determination of the locus of application). The author (Mufti Muhammad Taqi Usmani) is summarizing the rules of issuing fatwas and explains that this type of ijtihad is ongoing and necessary. Imam al-Shatibi’s quote begins by stating that ijtihad is of two types: one that cannot cease until the end of the world (tahqiq al-manat) and another that can cease. The first type involves applying a fixed Sharia ruling to specific instances where the ruling's conditions are met but the actual subjects need identification. For example, the Quran commands witnesses who are 'just' ('adl), but determining who is just requires ijtihad because justice varies among individuals. This type of ijtihad is essentially applying the universal (kulli) to its particulars (juz’iyyat). The author then includes a footnote about Imam al-Shatibi's biography and a clarification about another scholar with a similar name. A second footnote from Shaykh Abdullah Darraz explains that tahqiq al-manat is not qiyas but pure application of the universal. On page 160, the author continues emphasizing that in many legal scenarios—such as identifying the poor for bequests, calculating maintenance, or evaluating witnesses—taqlid (blind imitation) is impossible because one must first determine the factual circumstances (manat) before applying the ruling. This ijtihad requires effort (wus‘) and is necessary for judges, muftis, and even individual laypeople in their own affairs. The passage ends with a note that ijtihad would never cease; the final words are cut off.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): Striving to derive or apply a Sharia ruling by exerting one’s intellectual capacity
تحقيق المناط (tahqiq al-manat): Verifying the presence of the effective cause or condition (manat) in a specific case to which a general ruling applies
التقليد (al-taqlid): Accepting the ruling of a qualified jurist without knowing its evidence

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the passage, why is taqlid (blind imitation) impossible in cases like determining who qualifies as 'poor' for a bequest?
A) Because the rulings on bequests are not based on Sharia texts.
B) Because the specific case (manat) has not been determined yet and requires ijtihad.
C) Because taqlid is always forbidden in Hanafi fiqh.
D) Because the mufti must personally witness the recipients.

Key Takeaway
Tahqiq al-manat is the ongoing, indispensable ijtihad of applying universal Sharia rulings to the specific facts of each case, ensuring that fatwas and judgments are correctly grounded.

Why a Mufti Needs Ijtihad (Even as a Muqallid)
Rational argument for ijtihad's necessity in fatwa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a mufti who follows a madhhab must still exercise ijtihad when issuing a fatwa on a new case.

Core Idea
A mufti cannot rely on taqlid alone because each new case requires determining its legal basis (manat), which is an act of ijtihad.

This passage from Mufti Muhammad Taqi Usmani's summary of Rasm al-Mufti presents a key argument: the very nature of fatwa demands ijtihad. Even a mufti who is a muqallid (follower of a madhhab) must exert independent reasoning to apply general Shari'ah principles to specific, unprecedented situations. Using examples like bequests and family maintenance, the author shows that taqlid is insufficient because the basis of the ruling (manat) is not yet established.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Main Claim
The passage states that a mufti needs ijtihad even if he is a muqallid. Pinpoint this claim in the text.
Look at the first sentence: 'أصول الإفتاء لا بد فيه من بلوغ حد الوسع، وهو الاجتهاد.'
2
Understand the Reason: Taqlid Requires Established Manat
Taqlid is only possible after the basis (manat) of the ruling is established. In new cases, the manat is unknown, so ijtihad is required.
The author says: 'لأن التقليد إنما يتصوّر بعد تحقيق مناط الحكم المقلد فيه والمناط هنا لم يتحقق بعد.'
3
Analyze the Examples
Examine the bequest to the poor and maintenance for wives. In each, the mufti must assess specific conditions to determine the ruling.
For bequest: who is 'poor'? For maintenance: what is the condition of the supporter and supported?
4
Recognize the Precedent Issue
Even if a similar case exists, the mufti must verify whether the current case matches the precedent—this verification is also ijtihad.
The text says: 'وكذلك إن فرضنا أنه تقدم لنا مثلها، فلا بد من النظر في كونها مثلها أو لا، وهو نظر اجتهادي أيضاً.'
5
Conclude the Necessity
The mufti cannot dispense with ijtihad because each fatwa case is unique in its particulars, and the Shari'ah provides general rules, not specific rulings for every instance.
This conclusion sets the stage for the rest of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تلخيص قواعد رسم المفتي
أصول الإفتاء لا بد فيه من بلوغ حد الوسع، وهو الاجتهاد. فهذا مما يفتقر إليه الحاكم في كل شاهد، كما إذا أوصى بماله للفقراء؛ فلا شك أن من الناس من لا شيء له، فيتحقق فيه اسم الفقر، فهو من أهل الوصيّة، ومنهم من لا حاجة به ولا فقر، وإن لم يملك نصاباً، وبينهما وسائط... فينظر فيه هل الغالب عليه حكم الفقر أو حكم الغنى؟ وكذلك فى فَرْض نفقات الزوجات والقرابات، إذ هو مفتقر إلى النظر في حال المنفق عليه والمنفق، وحال الوقت، إلى غير ذلك من الأمور التي لا تنضبط بحصر، ولا يمكن استيفاء القول في آحادها، فلا يمكن أن يُستغنى ههنا بالتقليد، لأن التقليد إنما يتصوّر بعد تحقيق مناط الحكم المقلد فيه والمناط هنا لم يتحقق بعد، لأن كل صورة من صوره النازلة نازلة مستأنفة في نفسها لم يتقدم لها نظير، وإن تقدم لها في نفس الأمر فلم يتقدم لنا، فلا بد من النظر فيها بالاجتهاد، وكذلك إن فرضنا أنه تقدم لنا مثلها، فلا بد من النظر في كونها مثلها أو لا، وهو نظر اجتهادي أيضاً ... ويكفيك من ذلك أنّ الشريعة لم تنص على حكم كل جزئية على حدتها، وإنما أتت بأمورٍ كُليّةٍ، وعبارات مطلقة تتناول أعداداً لا تنحصر، ومع ذلك، فلكل معيّن خصوصية ليست في غيره، ولو فى نفس التّعيين، وليس ما به الامتياز معتبراً فى الحكم بإطلاق ولا هو طردي بإطلاق بل ذلك منقسم إلى الضربين، وبينهما قسم ثالث يأخذ بجهة من الطرفين؛ فلا يبقى صورة من الصور الوجودية المعيّنة إلا وللعالم فيها نظر سهل أو صعب حتى يحقق تحت أي دليل تدخل، فإن أخذت بشبه من الطرفين، فالأمر أصعب، وهذا كله بين لمن شدا في العلم... فالحاصل أنه لا بد منه بالنسبة إلى كل ناظر وحاكم ومفت، بل بالنسبة إلى كلّ مكلّف في نفسه... ولو فرض ارتفاع هذا الاجتهاد، لم تتنزل الأحكام الشرعيّة على أفعال المكلفين إلا في الذهن؛ لأنها مُطلَقات وعمومات، وما يرجع إلى ذلك منزلات على أفعال مطلقات كذلك، والأفعال لا تقع في الوجود مطلقة، وإنما تقع معيّنة مشخصة، فلا يكون الحكم واقعاً عليها إلا بعد المعرفة بأنّ هذا المعين يشمله ذلك المطلق أو ذلك العام، وقد يكون ذلك سهلاً وقد لا يكون، وكله اجتهاد (۱)
والأمر الخامس: أنّ الفتوى قد تختلف باختلاف الأشخاص نظراً إلى خصوص أحوالهم، وباختلاف العُرفِ وأحوال الزمان حسب تحقيق المناط كما سيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى.
والأمر السادس: أن كثيراً من المسائل الجديدة تنشأ في كل عصر، وحدثت في عصرنا هذا بصفة خاصة، حيث تغيّرت مناهج الحياة إلى حد كبير عما كانت معهودةً فى عهد المجتهدين السابقين. فلا يوجد لمثل هذه المسائل ذكر صريح في كتبهم حتى يُنقل بعينه. وإنما يحتاج المفتى المعاصر في معرفة حكمها، إما إلى تنزيلها على العمومات الواردة في كلامهم، أو القياس والاستنباط من نظائرها وأشباهها. وإنّه عمل دقيق لابد له من بصيرة ثاقبة، وفهم المبادئ الشرعية فهماً صحيحاً.
ونظراً إلى هذه الأمور الستة، لابد للمفتى، وإن كان مقلدا، من أن تكون له بصيرة في جميع هذه الأمور. وإن مثل هذه البصيرة لا تحصل بمجرد مطالعة الكتب وحفظ جزئيات الفقه، وإنّما تحتاج إلى ملكة فقهيّة وتجربة لا تكاد تحصل إلا بممارسة الفتوى والتمرن عليها لدى أساتذة مهرة. ولذلك قالوا:
(1) الموافقات للشاطي رحمه الله تعالى - (٤ : ٨٩ إلى ٩٣)
Summary of the Rules of Rasm al-Mufti. The foundations of ifta' necessarily require reaching the utmost capacity, which is ijtihad. This is something that the judge needs in every witnessed case, such as when someone bequeaths his wealth to the poor. There is no doubt that among people there is one who owns nothing, so the name of poverty applies to him, and he is eligible for the bequest. And among them is one who has no need or poverty, even if he does not own a nisab, and between them are intermediaries... So one examines: which predominantly applies — the ruling of poverty or the ruling of wealth? Likewise in the assessment of maintenance for wives and relatives, for it requires examining the condition of the one being supported and the supporter, and the condition of the time, and other matters that cannot be confined to enumeration, nor can discussion be exhausted in their particulars. So one cannot dispense with taqlid here, because taqlid is conceivable only after establishing the basis (manat) of the ruling being followed, and the basis here has not yet been established. Because every image of its occurring cases is a new case in itself for which no precedent has occurred. Even if a precedent had occurred in reality, it has not occurred for us. So it is necessary to examine it through ijtihad. Likewise, if we assume that a similar case has occurred for us, we must still examine whether it is indeed similar or not, and that is also an ijtihadi consideration. ... And sufficient for you is that the Shari'ah did not stipulate a ruling for every particular instance individually. Rather, it brought universal matters and absolute expressions that encompass countless numbers. Nonetheless, every specific instance has a particularity not found in another, even if only in the very specification. And the distinguishing feature is neither absolutely considered in the ruling nor absolutely accidental, but rather it is divided into the two types, and between them is a third type that takes from both sides. Thus there remains no specific existential image except that the scholar has an easy or difficult consideration in it, until he verifies under which evidence it falls. And if it takes resemblance from both sides, the matter is more difficult. This is all clear to one who is proficient in knowledge. So the conclusion is that it is necessary for every observer, judge, and mufti, nay for every legally responsible person regarding himself. And if we suppose this ijtihad were removed, the Shari'ah rulings would not descend upon the actions of the responsible except in the mind, because they are absolutes and generalities, and what pertains to that are similarly absolute actions. And actions do not occur in existence as absolutes; rather, they occur as finite and specific. So the ruling does not apply to them except after knowing that this finite thing is included by that absolute or that general. And that may be easy or may not, and all of it is ijtihad. (1)
The fifth matter: that the fatwa may differ according to the difference of persons, considering their specific circumstances, and according to differences of custom and conditions of time, based on verification of the basis (manat), as will come Insha'Allah.
The sixth matter: that many new issues arise in every age, and they have arisen in this age of ours in particular, as life's methods have changed greatly from what was customary in the era of earlier mujtahids. So there is no explicit mention of such issues in their books such that it could be transmitted verbatim. Rather, the contemporary mufti, in knowing their ruling, needs either to apply them to the generalities found in their statements, or to use analogy (qiyas) and inference (istinbat) from their parallels and similars. And this is a precise work that requires keen insight and a correct understanding of the Shari'ah principles.
In light of these six matters, the mufti, even if he is a muqallid, must have insight in all these matters. And such insight is not obtained merely by studying books and memorizing the particulars of fiqh, but rather requires a juristic faculty and experience that can hardly be obtained except by practicing fatwa and training under skilled teachers. Therefore they said:
(1) Al-Muwafaqat by al-Shatibi, may Allah have mercy on him, 4:89-93.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a rational argument demonstrating why a mufti — even one who primarily follows the Hanafi school (muqallid) — must exercise ijtihad. The author, Mufti Muhammad Taqi Usmani, is summarizing rules for the mufti's methodology. He begins by stating that the very basis of ifta' requires reaching a level of capacity that is ijtihad. He gives examples: a bequest to the poor requires determining who qualifies as poor; maintenance for wives requires assessing the circumstances of both parties and the time. These determinations are not covered by simple taqlid because taqlid presupposes that the basis (manat) of the ruling is already established. But here the basis is not yet established; each case is new and requires fresh scrutiny. Moreover, even if a precedent exists, one must verify that the current case matches the precedent — which itself is ijtihad. The author then makes a deeper point: the Shari'ah gives general principles and absolute statements, not a specific ruling for every particular. Each particular has its own specificity, and the distinguishing feature may or may not be relevant. So every actual case requires a scholar to exert ijtihad to determine which evidence covers it. If the case resembles two categories, it is even harder. The author concludes that ijtihad is necessary for every observer, judge, and mufti, indeed for every accountable person. Then he notes that fatwa varies with persons, customs, and times. New issues constantly arise, and the contemporary mufti cannot find explicit rulings in classical texts; he must apply general principles or use qiyas. Therefore, even a muqallid mufti must have insight (basira) that comes only from practice and training, not just book knowledge. The passage is sourced from al-Shatibi's al-Muwafaqat.

Key Terms
اجتهاد (ijtihad): The maximum effort in deriving legal rulings from the sources; independent reasoning.
تقليد (taqlid): Following the ruling of a mujtahid without knowing his evidence.
مناط الحكم (manat al-hukm): The basis or occasioning factor for a legal ruling; the specific situation to which a ruling applies.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can a mufti not rely solely on taqlid when issuing a fatwa?
A) Because taqlid is forbidden in the Hanafi school.
B) Because each new case requires verification of the basis (manat) to apply the correct ruling.
C) Because a mufti must be a mujtahid mutlaq.
D) Because taqlid only applies to acts of worship, not to transactions.

Key Takeaway
A mufti must exercise ijtihad because each fatwa case is a new particular requiring verification of its legal basis, not just transmission of a previous ruling.

Second Principle: Single Opinion Must Be Taken
Rule that a single Hanafi opinion is binding regardless of its transmission category

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the second principle of Hanafi fatwa and apply it to determine which rulings are binding when only one opinion exists.

Core Idea
A single Hanafi opinion is binding regardless of its transmission category or source authority.

This lesson introduces the second foundational principle for Hanafi muftis. It clarifies that when only one ruling exists from any level of Hanafi authority—whether from the core authoritative books (Zahir al-Riwayah), the rare transmissions (Nawadir), or later fatwas (Waqi'at)—it is obligatory to adopt it. The only exception is when the ruling's effective cause (illah) is clearly no longer present.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key rule
The mufti must follow the sole opinion in a matter. There is no freedom to ignore it based on its category.
2
Recognize the scope
The rule applies whether the opinion is from Zahir al-Riwayah, Nawadir, or later fatwas. The classification into categories only matters when there are multiple opinions.
3
Understand the exception
If it is patently obvious that the ruling's effective cause (illah) has ceased—e.g., a custom has changed—then the ruling may not be binding. This exception will be detailed later.
4
Apply the principle
In practice, when only one Hanafi ruling exists on an issue, the mufti must adopt it without considering its hierarchical level, unless the cause is absent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الثاني
إذا كانت المسألة ليس فيها إلا قول واحد للفقهاء الحنفية المتقدمين منهم والمتأخرين، تعيّن الأخذ به. إن تقسيم المسائل إلى طبقات مختلفة إنما يؤثر في المسائل التي وجدت فيها أقوال مختلفة فى المذهب، فأما إذا لم يكن في المسئلة إلا قول واحد، يتعيّن الأخذ به سواء أكانت تلك المسئلة من ظاهر الرواية أم من النوادر أو الواقعات والفتاوى، إلا إذا عُلم بالبداهة أنّ تلك المسئلة معلولة بعلة قد فقدت كما سيأتي بيانه إن شاء الله تعالى.
The second principle: If a matter has only one statement from the Hanafi jurists—both early and later—it is obligatory to adopt it. The division of issues into different categories only affects matters in which different statements exist in the school. As for a matter in which there is only one statement, it is obligatory to adopt it, whether that matter is from the Zahir al-Riwayah, the Nawadir, or the Waqi'at and Fatawa, unless it is known by obviousness that that matter is caused by a ratio legis that has ceased, as will be explained later, Allah willing.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This is the second foundational principle (al-asl al-thani) for Hanafi muftis. It establishes that when only one ruling exists from Hanafi scholars—both early (mutaqaddimin) and later (muta'akhkhirin)—the mufti must follow it. The classification of rulings into Zahir al-Riwayah, Nawadir, and Fatawa/Waqi'at only matters when there are conflicting opinions. A single statement is binding regardless of its category. The exception is when it is patently obvious that the ruling was based on an effective cause (illah) that no longer applies—e.g., a custom that has changed. This previews a later discussion. The rule prevents unnecessary speculation and ensures consistency in fatwa.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Zāhir al-Riwāyah): The transmitted rulings from the six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, considered the most authoritative.
نوادر (Nawādir): Rare transmissions not in the six books, e.g., from Hasan ibn Ziyad.
واقعات وفتاوى (Wāqi‘āt wa Fatāwā): Later rulings and case-based fatwas from Hanafi scholars after the founding imams.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second principle, when must a mufti follow a ruling from the Nawadir?
A) Only if there is no ruling in Zahir al-Riwayah
B) When it is the only ruling in the school on that issue
C) Never, because Nawadir are not reliable
D) Only if later scholars have endorsed it

Key Takeaway
A single Hanafi opinion is binding on the mufti regardless of its source category, except when its underlying cause is clearly absent.

The Third Principle: Hierarchy of Preference in Conflicting Opinions
Rule for muftis on resolving conflicting opinions within the Hanafi school.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi hierarchy to determine the mufta bihi when opinions conflict, distinguishing between the role of a mujtahid and a non-mujtahid mufti.

Core Idea
When opinions conflict, follow the later choice of Abu Hanifa, then Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad; a non-mujtahid mufti must always follow Abu Hanifa.

This passage lays down the foundational hierarchy for resolving internal Hanafi disputes. It is essential knowledge for any mufti operating within the school. The rule balances the authority of the Imam with the flexibility needed for those capable of independent reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of conflict
Determine if there are two opinions or transmissions from a single imam. For Abu Hanifa, prefer the later one or his explicit choice.
2
Descend to disciples
If no choice is known from Abu Hanifa, follow the choices of the disciples in order: first Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad.
3
Special case: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
If there is a conflict between the choice of Abu Hanifa and that of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), check the mufti's ijtihad capacity.
4
Apply based on ijtihad capacity
If the mufti is a mujtahid, he may choose; if he is a muqallid (non-mujtahid), he must follow Abu Hanifa's choice.
This rule preserves the primacy of the Imam's authority for non-mujtahids.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الثالث
إذا كان فى المسئلة قولان أو روايتان عن الإمام أبي حنيفة، أخذ بالآخر منهما، أو بما ثبت اختياره من قبل الإمام. وإن لم يثبت منه اختيار، عُمِل بما اختاره الإمام أبو يوسف، ثمّ بما اختاره الإمام محمد، ثم بما اختاره زفر و الحسن بن زياد رحمهم الله تعالى. أما إذا كان هناك اختلاف بين اختيار أبى حنيفة، واختيار صاحبيه، فإن كان المفتى من أهل الاجتهاد يتخيّر، وإن لم يكن من أهل الاجتهاد، يأخذ بقول الإمام أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى.
The third principle: If there are two statements or two transmissions from Imam Abu Hanifa, take the later of the two, or that which is established as his choice. If no choice is established from him, act according to what Imam Abu Yusuf chose, then what Imam Muhammad chose, then what Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad chose—may Allah have mercy on them. As for when there is a difference between the choice of Abu Hanifa and the choice of his two companions, then if the mufti is a person of ijtihad, he may choose; if he is not a person of ijtihad, he takes the statement of Imam Abu Hanifa.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This third principle (al-asl al-thalith) provides a detailed hierarchy for resolving conflicts within the Hanafi school. When two transmissions from Abu Hanifa himself exist, the later one is preferred, or whichever he explicitly chose. If no choice is known, we follow the choices of the major disciples in order: first Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar and Hasan ibn Ziyad. A special case arises when there is disagreement between Abu Hanifa's preference and that of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Here, the mufti's own ijtihad capacity matters: if he is a mujtahid, he can weigh the evidences and choose; if not (i.e., a muqallid mufti), he must follow Abu Hanifa's position. This rule preserves the primacy of the Imam's authority for non-mujtahids while allowing flexibility for those capable of ijtihad.

Key Terms
اختيار (ikhtiyār): The deliberate preference (choice) of an imam among multiple possible rulings; an explicit selection.
صاحبين (al-Ṣāḥibayn): Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, the two main disciples of Abu Hanifa.
زفر (Zufar): Imam Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl, a prominent Hanafi jurist and student of Abu Hanifa.
الحسن بن زياد (al-Ḥasan ibn Ziyād): Imam al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, a Hanafi jurist and student of Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third principle, if a non-mujtahid mufti finds that Abu Hanifa chose one ruling but the Sahibayn chose a different one, what must he do?
A) Follow the Sahibayn because they are two
B) Follow Abu Hanifa's choice
C) Choose whichever seems more sound
D) Refer the question to a higher authority

Key Takeaway
The third principle establishes a clear hierarchy: the later or chosen opinion of Abu Hanifa takes precedence, then Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, and in case of direct conflict between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn, a non-mujtahid mufti must follow Abu Hanifa.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

The Third Principle: Hierarchy of Conflicting Hanafi Rulings
Explains the hierarchy for choosing among conflicting Hanafi rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Third Principle's hierarchy to determine which opinion to give in fatwa when conflicting narrations exist, and identify which state a given scenario falls under.

Core Idea
Conflicting rulings from Abu Hanifa are resolved by a strict hierarchy: latest opinion first, then his disciples' choices.

When multiple rulings appear in the Hanafi school, the mufti must know which one to adopt. The Third Principle provides a clear ladder of preference. This lesson unpacks that ladder and the three states of conflicting narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of conflict
Determine whether the conflict involves two narrations from Abu Hanifa, between Abu Hanifa and his companions, or among companions.
The principle and states each address a different type of conflict.
2
Apply the sequence of preference
First check if Abu Hanifa had a later opinion or a chosen view. If established, use it. If not, move to the choices of Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, and al-Hasan in that order.
For non-mujtahid muftis, Abu Hanifa's view always precedes his companions' choices.
3
Classify the scenario into one of the three states
Determine which state applies: (1) Abu Hanifa retracted an earlier view, (2) he had no preference between two views, (3) companions' views are all narrations from him. This affects whether you use the later view or the companions' order.
State 2 invokes the hierarchy of disciples; State 3 affirms that all disciples' views are ultimately from Abu Hanifa.
4
Consider the mufti's level of ijtihad
If the mufti is a mujtahid, he may choose between Abu Hanifa and his companions; otherwise, he must follow Abu Hanifa.
This only applies when there is a direct conflict between Abu Hanifa and his companions.

الأصل الثالث: إذا كان فى المسئلة قولان أو روايتان عن الإمام أبي حنيفة، أخذ بالآخر منهما، أو بما ثبت اختياره من قبل الإمام. وإن لم يثبت منه اختيار، عُمِل بما اختاره الإمام أبو يوسف، ثمّ بما اختاره الإمام محمد، ثم بما اختاره زفر و الحسن بن زياد رحمهم الله تعالى. أما إذا كان هناك اختلاف بين اختيار أبى حنيفة، واختيار صاحبيه، فإن كان المفتى من أهل الاجتهاد يتخيّر، وإن لم يكن من أهل الاجتهاد، يأخذ بقول الإمام أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى.
الواقع أنه قد تُنسب إلى الإمام أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى أقوال أو روايات كثيرة ربّما تبدو متعارضة وله حالات
الحالة الأولى: أنّ الإمام رحمه الله تعالى ذهب إلى قول في بداية الأمر، ثمّ رجع عن قوله ذلك إلى قوله الآخر، كما روى عنه في مسئلة الوضوء بالنبيذ وغيرها. فالأصل أن يؤخذ بقوله الأخير الذى رجع إليه.
والحالة الثانية: ماذكره ابن عابدین رحمه الله تعالى من أنه قد لا يترجح عنده أحد القولين، فيستوى رأيه فيهما. ولذا تراهم يحكون عنه في مسئلة القولين على وجه يُفيد تساويهما عنده فيقولون وفى المسئلة وإن لم يثبت عن الإمام اختيار أحدِ القولين وترجيحه في مثل هذا، فالأصل الذي مشى عليه فقهاء الحنفيّة، وذكره ابن عابدين في منظومة أنه يؤخذ بما اختاره الإمام أبويوسف، وإن لم يثبت منه اختيار، يؤخذ بما اختاره الإمام محمد، ثمّ بما اختاره الإمام زفر والحسن بن زياد رحمهم الله رسم المفتى جميعا، فهما في مرتبة واحدة ، وقيل زفر مقدم على الحسن بن زياد.
والحالة الثالثة: ماثبت عن أصحابه من الإمام أبي يوسف ومحمد وزفر والحسن بن زياد رحمهم الله تعالى من أنهم لم يقولوا قولاً إلا وهو رواية عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى. وقد نقل ابن عابدين رحمه الله تعالى عن الحاوى القدسيّ: "رُوى" عن جميع أصحابه الكبار كأبي يوسف ومحمد وزفر والحسن أنهم قالوا: ما قلنا في مسئلة قولاً إلا وهو روايتنا عن أبي حنيفة، وأقسموا عليه أيماناً غلاظاً، فلم يتحقق إذن في الفقه جواب ولا مذهب إلا له كيف ما كان وما نُسب إلى غيره إلا بطريق المجاز للموافقة.
The Third Principle: If there are two sayings or two narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa in a matter, the later of them is taken, or that which is established as his chosen view. If his choice is not established, one acts upon what Imam Abu Yusuf chose, then upon what Imam Muhammad chose, then upon what Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad chose. However, if there is a disagreement between the choice of Abu Hanifa and the choice of his two companions, then if the mufti is from the people of ijtihad, he may choose; if he is not from the people of ijtihad, he takes the saying of Imam Abu Hanifa.
The reality is that many sayings or narrations are attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa that may seem contradictory, and they have states:
First state: The Imam initially held one opinion, then retracted that opinion in favor of another, as narrated concerning the issue of wudu with nabidh and others. The principle is to take his later opinion to which he returned.
Second state: What Ibn Abidin mentioned, that sometimes he does not give preponderance to one of the two sayings, so his view is balanced between them. Thus they narrate from him about an issue with two sayings in a way that indicates their equality in his view. If the Imam's choice and preponderance of one saying is not established, the principle upon which the Hanafi jurists have proceeded (mentioned by Ibn Abidin in his poem) is that one takes what Imam Abu Yusuf chose; if his choice is not established, one takes what Imam Muhammad chose; then what Imam Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad chose – they are on the same level, and it is said Zufar precedes al-Hasan ibn Ziyad.
Third state: What is established from his companions – Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad – that they never said any opinion except that it is a narration from Abu Hanifa. Ibn Abidin reported from al-Hawi al-Qudsi that it was narrated from all his senior companions that they said: 'We never said an opinion on any issue except that it is our narration from Abu Hanifa', and they took strong oaths on that. Thus, there is no verified answer or madhhab in fiqh except from him, and what is attributed to others is only in a metaphorical sense of agreement.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents the Third Principle and its detailed states. The Third Principle establishes a clear hierarchy for when there are conflicting narrations from Abu Hanifa: first prefer the later opinion of Abu Hanifa; if unknown, then the choice of Abu Yusuf; then Muhammad; then Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad. If there is a difference between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), a mujtahid mufti may choose, but a non-mujtahid must follow Abu Hanifa. The three states explain: (1) When Abu Hanifa himself retracted his earlier view, his later view is taken. (2) When he had no preference between two views, we follow the choices of his disciples in order. (3) All opinions from the major disciples are actually narrations from Abu Hanifa himself, so there is no genuine ijtihad apart from his. This underscores the unity of the Hanafi school: all authoritative rulings trace back to the Imam.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد (al-Ijtihad): The ability to derive rulings from evidence; a mufti who possesses this may choose among conflicting views.
اختيار (ikhtiyar): The preferred or chosen view of a scholar among available opinions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Third Principle, if Imam Abu Hanifa's later opinion is unknown, and neither Abu Yusuf nor Muhammad chose a view, but Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad have different choices, which is taken?
A) The view of Abu Hanifa's earlier opinion
B) The view of Zufar, and al-Hasan's view is not considered
C) The view of Zufar, and if conflicting, Zufar is preferred over al-Hasan ibn Ziyad
D) The mufti may choose between the views of Zufar and al-Hasan

Key Takeaway
The Third Principle ensures consistency in Hanafi fatwa by establishing a clear hierarchy: the latest view of Abu Hanifa, then the choices of his disciples in order.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
6 min
Hierarchy for Conflicting Narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa
Rules for mufti when multiple conflicting statements from Imam Abu Hanifa exist

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the hierarchy for preferring between conflicting narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa to determine the mufta bihi in a given case.

Core Idea
When no retraction or preference, follow: Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar and Hasan ibn Ziyad.

This lesson addresses a crucial practical skill for a mufti: what to do when multiple seemingly contradictory narrations are attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa. The Hanafi school has developed a clear hierarchy to resolve such conflicts, based on the principles of rasm al-mufti, ensuring consistency and authority in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The passage presents three distinct cases regarding conflicting narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa: retraction, equal positions, and student attribution. Recognize each scenario's characteristics.
Focus on the practical implications for a mufti, not the historical details.
2
Apply the retraction rule
If Imam Abu Hanifa retracted an earlier opinion, the later opinion is authoritative. Confirm whether a clear retraction is known.
The example of wudu with nabidh illustrates this.
3
Apply the student hierarchy
If no retraction and no preference from the Imam, follow the hierarchy: first Abu Yusuf's chosen opinion, then Muhammad's, then Zufar's (preferred over Hasan) or Hasan's.
This hierarchy is the golden rule for muftis in such cases.
4
Understand the third scenario
The major students swore their opinions were narrations from Abu Hanifa. This explains how multiple opinions originate but does not change the hierarchy; it only reinforces the Imam's role.
This scenario provides context but does not override the practical rules above.
5
Practice applying the hierarchy
Given a hypothetical conflicting narration, trace through the steps: check for retraction, then check for preference, then apply the student hierarchy.
Use the fatwa hierarchy block as a checklist.

الواقع أنه قد تُنسب إلى الإمام أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى أقوال أو روايات كثيرة ربّما تبدو متعارضة وله حالات

الحالة الأولى: أنّ الإمام رحمه الله تعالى ذهب إلى قول في بداية الأمر، ثمّ رجع عن قوله ذلك إلى قوله الآخر، كما روى عنه في مسئلة الوضوء بالنبيذ وغيرها. فالأصل أن يؤخذ بقوله الأخير الذى رجع إليه.

والحالة الثانية: ماذكره ابن عابدین رحمه الله تعالى من أنه قد لا يترجح عنده أحد القولين، فيستوى رأيه فيهما. ولذا تراهم يحكون عنه في مسئلة القولين على وجه يُفيد تساويهما عنده فيقولون وفى المسئلة وإن لم يثبت عن الإمام اختيار أحدِ القولين وترجيحه في مثل هذا، فالأصل الذي مشى عليه فقهاء الحنفيّة، وذكره ابن عابدين في منظومة أنه يؤخذ بما اختاره الإمام أبويوسف، وإن لم يثبت منه اختيار، يؤخذ بما اختاره الإمام محمد، ثمّ بما اختاره الإمام زفر والحسن بن زياد رحمهم الله جميعا، فهما في مرتبة واحدة، وقيل زفر مقدم على الحسن بن زياد.

والحالة الثالثة: ماثبت عن أصحابه من الإمام أبي يوسف ومحمد وزفر والحسن بن زياد رحمهم الله تعالى من أنهم لم يقولوا قولاً إلا وهو رواية عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى. وقد نقل ابن عابدين رحمه الله تعالى عن الحاوى القدسيّ: "رُوى" عن جميع أصحابه الكبار كأبي يوسف ومحمد وزفر والحسن أنهم قالوا: ما قلنا في مسئلة قولاً إلا وهو روايتنا عن أبي حنيفة، وأقسموا عليه أيماناً غلاظاً، فلم يتحقق إذن في الفقه جواب ولا مذهب إلا له كيف ما كان وما نُسب إلى غيره إلا بطريق المجاز للموافقة.

ومعناه على ما حققه العلامة الكوثري رحمه الله تعالى أن الإمام أبا حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى كان يُبدى أمام تلامذته احتمالات مختلفة في مسئلة واحدة، وكان أصحابه يأخذون بأحد هذه الاحتمالات. فكأن كل واحد منها رواية عنه، لأنه هو الذى أثار تلك الاحتمالات بأدلتها. ونحكى هنا كلام العلامة الكوثري رحمه الله تعالى بلفظه لما فيه من فوائد قال رحمه الله تعالى "ومنشأ ادعاء أن تلك الأقوال كلّها أقوال أبي حنيفة، هو ما كان يجرى عليه أبو حنيفة في تفقيه أصحابه من احتجاجه لأحد الأحكام المحتملة في مسألة وانتصاره له بأدلّة، ثمّ كرّروه بالردّ عليه بنقض أدلته، وبترجيحه الاحتمال الثاني بأدلّةٍ أخرى، ثمّ نقضها بترجيح احتمال ثالث بأدلة تدريباً لأصحابه على التفقه على خطوات ومراحل إلى أن يستقر الحكم المتعين في نهاية التمحيص، ويدوّن في الديوان فى عداد المسائل الممحصة، فمنهم من ترجح عنده غير ما استقر عليه الأمر من تلك الأقوال باجتهاده الخاص، فيكون هذا المترجح عنده قوله من وجه، وقول أبي حنيفة من وجه آخر، من حيث إنّه هو الذى أثار هذا الاحتمال ودلل عليه أولاً و إن عدل عنه أخيراً. ومصداق ذلك ما أخرجه ابن أبى العَوَّام ) عن محمد بن أحمد بن حماد بن محمد بن عبد الله
The reality is that many sayings or narrations are attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, which may appear contradictory. He has (three) cases:

First case: The Imam, may Allah have mercy on him, initially held one opinion, then he retracted that opinion and adopted another, as narrated from him in the issue of wudu with nabidh (date wine) and others. The principle is to take his later opinion to which he retracted.

Second case: What Ibn Abidin, may Allah have mercy on him, mentioned: that sometimes neither of the two opinions is preponderant in his view, so his opinion is balanced between them. Thus you see them narrating about him in an issue of two opinions in a manner indicating they are equal in his view. They say: 'And in the issue, if no choice or preference of one of the two opinions is established from the Imam, then in such a case, the principle upon which the Hanafi jurists have proceeded, and which Ibn Abidin mentioned in his didactic poem, is that one takes what Imam Abu Yusuf chose; if no choice is established from him, one takes what Imam Muhammad chose; then what Imam Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad chose—may Allah have mercy on them all—and they are both on the same rank. It is also said that Zufar is given precedence over al-Hasan ibn Ziyad.'

Third case: What is established from his students, Imams Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, may Allah have mercy on them, that they did not utter any opinion except that it is a narration from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. Ibn Abidin, may Allah have mercy on him, transmitted from al-Hawi al-Qudsi: 'It is narrated from all his senior students, such as Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, and al-Hasan, that they said: "We have not said any opinion in any issue except that it is our narration from Abu Hanifa," and they swore solemn oaths upon that. Thus, in fiqh, no answer or madhhab is realized except from him, however it may be, and what is attributed to others is only figuratively due to agreement.'

Its meaning, as verified by Allamah al-Kawthari, may Allah have mercy on him, is that Imam Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, would present to his students various possible views on a single issue, and his students would adopt one of these possibilities. So each of them is like a narration from him, because he was the one who raised those possibilities with their evidence. We quote here the words of Allamah al-Kawthari, may Allah have mercy on him, in his own words for the benefits they contain. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'The origin of the claim that all those statements are statements of Abu Hanifa is what Abu Hanifa would practice in teaching his students: arguing for one of the possible rulings on an issue and championing it with evidence, then they would respond to him by refuting his evidence and preferring the second possibility with other evidence, then refuting that by preferring a third possibility with evidence—training his students in fiqh step by step until the determined ruling settles at the end of scrutiny and is recorded in the register among the thoroughly examined issues. Among them, some would, by their own ijtihad, prefer something other than what the matter settled upon. Thus, what is preferred by him is his own statement in one sense, and Abu Hanifa's statement in another sense, since it was Abu Hanifa who first raised this possibility and supported it with evidence, even if he later retracted it. Evidence for this is what Ibn Abi al-Awwam narrated from Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Hammad ibn Muhammad ibn Abdullah...'
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage addresses a common challenge for muftis: how to handle multiple conflicting narrations attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa. The author, Mufti Muhammad Taqi Usmani, summarizes three scenarios from the Hanafi tradition. 

First scenario: When Imam Abu Hanifa himself retracted a statement, the later statement is authoritative. This is straightforward—the rule is to follow his final view. 

Second scenario: When the Imam held two opinions equally, with no preference, the mufti must follow a hierarchy among the major students. The relied-upon rule, as stated by Ibn Abidin, is: if Abu Yusuf chose one opinion, take it; otherwise, follow Muhammad's choice; then Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, with Zufar being given precedence over al-Hasan according to one view. This hierarchy reflects the scholarly weight of these early Hanafi imams. 

Third scenario: The major students—Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, and al-Hasan—swore that every opinion they expressed was a narration from Abu Hanifa. Allamah al-Kawthari explains that Imam Abu Hanifa would teach by presenting multiple possibilities and arguing for them, then his students would adopt some of these possibilities based on their own ijtihad. Thus, even if a student's view differs from the final settled view of Abu Hanifa, it still originates from the Imam's teaching. This does not mean all opinions carry equal weight; rather, it explains the phenomenon of multiple attributed opinions.

Key takeaway for a mufti: When faced with conflicting reports from Abu Hanifa, first check if the Imam retracted. If not, and no preference is known, follow the hierarchy of his students. The later explanation by al-Kawthari adds depth but does not change the practical rule.

Key Terms
الأصل (al-asl): The established rule or principle to be followed by the mufti.
رسم المفتي (rasm al-mufti): The set of rules and conventions governing the fatwa process, especially regarding hierarchy of sources and resolution of conflicting transmissions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if Imam Abu Hanifa has two equally balanced opinions and no preference is known from him?
A) The mufti must choose the easier opinion.
B) The mufti must follow the opinion of Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar and al-Hasan.
C) The mufti must follow the opinion of Imam Muhammad only.
D) The mufti must refer to Imam al-Shafi'i's opinion.

Key Takeaway
When no retraction or personal preference is known from Imam Abu Hanifa, the mufti follows a hierarchy: Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Mufti's Choice Between Imam and Students' Opinions
Story and rules on mufti's choice of opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a mufti who is a mujtahid within the madhhab may choose between differing opinions, whereas a mufti who is a muqallid must follow the rajih opinions.

Core Idea
The mufti's ijtihad level determines whether he may choose or must follow.

This passage presents a key principle of Hanafi ifta from the story of Imam Abu Hanifa's training. It defines when a mufti may exercise personal choice and when he must adhere to the rulings of earlier masters. The story illustrates how Imam Abu Hanifa trained his companions by raising multiple possibilities; then the passage extracts the rule for muftis facing differing opinions within the school.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the story of Imam Abu Hanifa's training
Read how Imam Abu Hanifa engaged his students in dialectic, raised multiple possibilities, and finally settled on one correct opinion. Note that he invented a third possibility to demonstrate the method.
The story shows that ijtihad involves exploring all valid perspectives before concluding.
2
Extract the principle: opinions of companions vs Imam
Understand that what settled with the Imam became his madhhab, and what settled with a companion was attributed to that companion. This sets the stage for when later opinions diverge.
This is the source of intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
3
Identify the three positions on mufti's choice
List the three views: (1) follow only Imam, (2) mufti free to choose any, (3) mufti chooses if mujtahid, otherwise follows Imam. Note that the third is deemed correct.
This is the core rule of rasm al-mufti.
4
Apply the third position: ijtihad within madhhab
Define 'mujtahid fi al-madhhab' using al-Bayri's explanation: capable of takhrij (deriving aspects) or tarjih (preferring opinions). The muqallid lacks this ability.
This determines the mufti's authority.
5
Apply the Fourth Principle: muqallid follows rajih
Understand that the muqallid mufti must follow the opinions preferred (rajih) by the scholars of tarjih, not weak opinions.
This safeguards consistency in fatwa.

أصول الإفتاء

۱۷۲

تلخيص قواعد رسم المفتي

فصاحوا به من نواحى الحلقة: يا أبا حنيفة بلدتك الغُربة. فقال لهم: رفقاً رفقاً! ماذا تقولون؟ قالوا: ليس هكذا القول قال: بحجة أم بغير حُجّة؟ قالوا: بل بحجة :قال هاتوا فناظرهم فغلبهم بالحجاج، حتى ردّهم إلى قوله وأذعنوا أنّ الخطأ منهم، فقال لهم: أعرفتم الآن ؟ قالوا: نعم. قال: فما تقولون فيمن يزعم أن قولكم هو الصواب، وأنّ هذا القول خطأ؟ قالوا: لا يكون ذلك، قد صح هذا القول، فناظَرَهم حتى ردّهم عن القول، فقالوا: يا أبا حنيفة! ظلمتنا، والصواب كان معنا. قال: فما تقولون فيمن يزعم أنّ هذا القول خطأً والأوّل خطأ، والصواب فى قول ثالث؟ فقالوا: هذا ما لا يكون. قال: فاستمعوا، واخترع قولاً ثالثاً، وناظرهم عليه حتى ردّهم إليه فأذعنوا وقالوا: يا أبا حنيفة علمنا. قال: الصواب هو القول الأول الذى أجبتكم به لعلّةٍ كذا وكذا، وهذه المسألة لا تخرج من هذه الثلاثة الأنحاء، ولكل منها وجة في الفقه ومذهب، وهذا الصواب فخذوه وارفضوا ما سواه. (۲) اهـ وهكذا كان تدريبه لأصحابه على الفقه وتمرينه على مدارج التفقه، فمثله يكون كثير الذكر للاحتمالات في المسائل، وقد يترجح عند هذا ما لا يترجح عند ذاك من أصحابه، فيكون هو مثير أغلب تلك الاحتمالات، فمُعْظَمُ تلك المسائل الخلافية من تذكير الإمام لأصح الأصحابه.

والحاصل أن أصحاب الإمام أبي حنيفة رحمهم الله تعالى إنّما اختاروا في كل مسئلةٍ من أحد الاحتمالات التي أثارها الإمام أبو حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى، ثمّ ما استقر عليه رأى الإمام صار مذهباً له، وما استقر عليه رأى أحد أصحابه، نُسب إليه.

وفي هذه الحالة، إن اختلف أقوال أصحابه عما استقر عليه رأى الإمام فإن فيه ثلاثة أقوال: الأول أنه يؤخذ بقول الإمام أبي حنيفة فقط، والثاني: أن المفتى مخيّر فى أخذِ ما شاء منها، والثالث: أنه إن كان المفتى أهل الاجتهاد، يتخيّر، وإن لم يكن من أهل الاجتهاد، يتبع قول الإمام. وهذا هو الصحيح. ونقل ابن عابدين عن البيري (1) رحمه الله تعالى قال: "والمراد بالاجتهاد أحد الاجتهادين، وهو مجتهد في المذهب، وغرف بأنه المتمكن من تخريج الوجوه على منصوص إمامه، أو المتبحر في مذهب إمامه، المتمكن من ترجيح قول له على آخر. (۲)

الأصل الرابع:
المفتى المقلد يُفتى بما رجّحه أصحاب الترجيح من مشايخ الحنفية، ولا يأخذ بالأقوال المرجوحة.

الهوامش:
(1) يعنون أن غربتك أى كونك فى غير وطنك أوقعتك في حيرة، إذ لم تتجه إلى الصواب. وفى تاج العروس: "بلد الرّجل تبيليداً إذا لم يتجه لشيئ، وبلد الإنسان إذا بخل و لم يجد، وبلد الرجل الحقته رة، وضرب بنفسه الأرض إعياء. وفى لسان العرب: "بلد الرّجل: إذا لم يتجه لشيئ وبلد: إذا نكس في العمل وضعف، حتى في الجري.
(۲) فضائل أبي حنيفة لابن أبى العوام ص ۱۱۱ فقره ۱۷۸
(۳) حسن التقاضى فى سيرة الإمام أبي يوسف القاضى رحمه الله تعالى ص ٦٠ إلى ٦٢
(1) العلامة البيري: هو إبراهيم بن حسين بن أحمد بن محمد بن بن أحمد بن بيري (نسبة إلى البيرة وهى موطنه مفتى مكة، ولد فى المدينة المنوّرة سنة ١٠٢٠ هـ وقال في خلاصة الأثر: "كانت ولادته في المدينة المنورة في نيف وعشرين وألف] وتوفّي (رحمه الله) بمكة سنة ١٠٩٩ هـ، ودفن بالمعلاة. كذا على ظهر النسخة المخطوطة لشرح العلامة البيري رحمه الله على الأشباه والنظائر.
(۲) شرح عقود رسم المفتى ص ٤٦
They shouted at him from all sides of the circle: 'O Abu Hanifa, your estrangement has bewildered you.' He said: 'Gently, gently! What are you saying?' They said: 'It is not like this.' He said: 'With proof or without proof?' They said: 'With proof.' He said: 'Bring it.' So he argued with them and overcame them, until he brought them back to his statement and they acknowledged that the error was from them. He said: 'Do you now know?' They said: 'Yes.' He said: 'What do you say about one who claims your statement is correct and this statement is wrong?' They said: 'That cannot be; this statement is proven correct.' So he argued until he brought them back from that statement. They said: 'You have wronged us; the correct was with us.' He said: 'What do you say about one who claims this statement is wrong and the first is wrong, and the correct is a third statement?' They said: 'This cannot be.' He said: 'Then listen.' He invented a third statement and argued with them until they acknowledged and said: 'Teach us.' He said: 'The correct is the first statement for such reason; this issue has three aspects, each having a face in fiqh. Take this correct one and discard what is besides it.' (2) Thus was his training of his companions in fiqh. So he would frequently mention possibilities, and what is preponderant with one may not be so with another. Thus he was the one who raised most possibilities. The conclusion: the companions chose from possibilities raised by the Imam; what settled with the Imam became his madhhab, and what settled with a companion was attributed to him. If their statements differ from the Imam's settled opinion, there are three views: first, take the Imam's statement only; second, the mufti may choose any; third, if the mufti is capable of ijtihad, he chooses, otherwise follows the Imam. This is correct. Ibn 'Abidin transmitted from al-Bayri: 'By ijtihad is meant being a mujtahid within the madhhab, capable of deriving aspects from the Imam's texts, or well-versed and able to prefer one statement over another.' (2) The Fourth Principle: The mufti who is a muqallid gives fatwa according to what the scholars of tarjih have preferred, and does not take weak statements. Footnotes: (1) Explanation of 'baladatka al-ghurba' from Taj al-'Arus and Lisan al-'Arab. (2) Reference to Fada'il Abi Hanifah. (3) Reference to Husn al-Taqadi. (1) Biography of al-Bayri. (2) Reference to Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage discusses the training method of Imam Abu Hanifa and the rules for muftis when dealing with differing opinions within the Hanafi school. It begins with a story illustrating how Imam Abu Hanifa taught his students by engaging them in dialectical reasoning, raising multiple possibilities for a single issue, and then demonstrating which was correct. This shows that the Hanafi school did not simply impose one view but developed through rigorous debate. The author then notes that the companions of Imam Abu Hanifa would select from the possibilities he raised; what settled with the Imam became his madhhab, and what settled with a companion was attributed to that companion. If later opinions of students differ from the Imam's settled view, there are three positions: (1) follow only the Imam, (2) the mufti may choose any, (3) the mufti if mujtahid chooses, otherwise follows the Imam. The third is deemed correct. Ibn 'Abidin clarifies that 'ijtihad' here means ijtihad within the madhhab (takhrij and tarjih). Finally, the Fourth Principle states that a muqallid mufti must follow the preferred opinions (rajih) of Hanafi scholars, not weak ones.

Key Terms
المجتهد في المذهب (al-mujtahid fi al-madhhab): One who can derive juristic aspects (takhrij) from the Imam's explicit texts or who is deeply versed and can prefer one statement over another (tarjih).
المفتى المقلد (al-mufti al-muqallid): A mufti who lacks the ability of ijtihad within the madhhab and must follow the preferred opinions of Hanafi scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should a mufti who is not a mujtahid within the madhhab do when faced with differing opinions between Imam Abu Hanifa and his students?
A) He may choose any opinion he wishes.
B) He must follow only the opinion of Imam Abu Hanifa.
C) He must follow the preferred (rajih) opinions of Hanafi scholars.
D) He must follow the opinion of the student who disagreed.

Key Takeaway
A mufti's authority to choose between differing opinions is contingent on his level of ijtihad within the madhhab; otherwise, he must follow the rajih opinions of earlier scholars.

Third Principle: Mufti's Choice Between Imam and Companions
Summarizing the third principle of Rasm al-Mufti

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the third principle of Rasm al-Mufti to determine whether a mufti must follow the Imam's settled opinion or may choose among the companions' opinions, based on his level of ijtihad fi al-madhhab.

Core Idea
A mufti must follow the Imam unless he is a mujtahid fi al-madhhab.

This passage from Ibn Abidin's summary of Rasm al-Mufti addresses a critical question: when Imam Abu Hanifa's final settled opinion differs from those of his distinguished companions, what should a mufti do? Three classical views existed, and the correct one depends entirely on the mufti's level of ijtihad. Understanding this principle safeguards the integrity of fatwa by ensuring that only qualified scholars venture beyond the Imam's explicit position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The Imam's final view differs from one or both of his companions. This is the pivot of the ikhtilaf.
2
List the three views
View 1: Only the Imam's opinion is taken. View 2: The mufti has free choice. View 3: If the mufti is a mujtahid fi al-madhhab, he chooses; otherwise he follows the Imam.
3
Understand the tarjih
The third view is declared sahih. Why? Because it respects both the Imam's authority and legitimate ijtihad within the school.
4
Define the key qualification
'Mujtahid fi al-madhhab' — one who can derive rulings from the Imam's texts and weigh between opinions. This is not absolute ijtihad.
5
Apply the rule
When giving fatwa, a non-mujtahid mufti must adhere to the Imam's view; a mujtahid fi al-madhhab may choose among the companions' views, but must do so based on tarjih, not whim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أن أصحاب الإمام أبي حنيفة رحمهم الله تعالى إنّما اختاروا في كل مسئلةٍ من أحد الاحتمالات التي أثارها الإمام أبو حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى، ثمّ ما استقر عليه رأى الإمام صار مذهباً له، وما استقر عليه رأى أحد أصحابه، نُسب إليه.
وفي هذه الحالة، إن اختلف أقوال أصحابه عما استقر عليه رأى الإمام فإن فيه ثلاثة أقوال: الأول أنه يؤخذ بقول الإمام أبي حنيفة فقط، والثاني: أن المفتى مخيّر فى أخذِ ما شاء منها، والثالث: أنه إن كان المفتى أهل الاجتهاد، يتخيّر، وإن لم يكن من أهل الاجتهاد، يتبع قول الإمام. وهذا هو الصحيح. ونقل ابن عابدين عن البيري رحمه الله تعالى قال: "والمراد وان بالاجتهاد أحد الاجتهادين، وهو مجتهد في المذهب، وغرف بأنه المتمكن من تخريج الوجوه على منصوص إمامه، أو المتبحر في مذهب إمامه، المتمكن من ترجيح قول له على آخر."
(۱) شرح عقود رسم المفتى ص ٤٦
And the result is that the companions of Imam Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on them) only chose in each issue from among the possibilities raised by Imam Abu Hanifa. Then what the Imam's opinion settled upon became his madhhab, and what settled upon the opinion of one of his companions was attributed to him. In this case, if the statements of his companions differ from what the Imam's opinion settled upon, there are three views: First, only the opinion of Imam Abu Hanifa is taken. Second, the mufti is given a choice to take whichever he wishes. Third, if the mufti is qualified for ijtihad, he chooses; if he is not qualified for ijtihad, he follows the Imam's opinion. This is the correct view. Ibn Abidin narrated from al-Bairi (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'The intended meaning of ijtihad here is one of two ijtihads: it is ijtihad in the madhhab, and it is known as one who is capable of deriving aspects from the nusus of his Imam, or one who is deeply learned in the madhhab of his Imam, capable of giving preponderance to one saying over another.' (1) Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti, p. 46
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage from Ibn Abidin's summary of the rules of Rasm al-Mufti deals with the third principle: when the Imam's own statements differ from those of his companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The author explains that the companions only made ijtihad in cases where the Imam had multiple possibilities. Once the Imam's final view was established, that became his madhhab. When the companions differ from that final view, there is a dispute among later scholars on what the mufti should do. Three views are recorded: (1) always follow Imam, (2) mufti has free choice, (3) distinction based on the mufti's level — if he is a mujtahid in the madhhab he can choose, otherwise he follows Imam. The third view is declared the correct (sahih) position. The definition of 'mujtahid in the madhhab' is given by al-Bairi: it means one who can derive rulings from the Imam's texts (takhrij al-wujuh) or is deeply versed and can weigh between different opinions. This is a crucial rule for the mufti: his level of ijtihad determines his freedom in choosing between conflicting reports from the school.

Key Terms
مجتهد في المذهب (mujtahid fi al-madhhab): One who can derive rulings from the Imam's nusus and weigh between opinions

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct view in the third principle, what should a non-mujtahid mufti do when the Imam's companions differ from his final settled opinion?
A) He may choose any opinion
B) He must follow the Imam's opinion
C) He follows the majority of companions
D) He refrains from giving fatwa

Key Takeaway
The mufti's level of ijtihad determines whether he may choose among conflicting opinions within the Hanafi school; without ijtihad fi al-madhhab, the Imam's settled view is binding.

Fourth Principle: Following the Preferred Views of the Rajjih Scholars
Expanding the fourth principle of Rasm al-Mufti

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the fourth principle of Rasm al-Mufti by identifying when the muqallid mufti must follow the tarjih of ashab al-tarjih over the default position of Imam Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
Muqallid mufti must follow the tarjih of the school's qualified scholars, not his own preference.

The Hanafi madhhab has a clear hierarchy of authority. While the default is to follow Imam Abu Hanifa's position, the senior scholars of tarjih (ashab al-tarjih) may prefer an alternative view from the companions like Abu Yusuf or Muhammad al-Shaybani. This lesson explains why they have this authority and why the muqallid mufti must obey their preferred opinion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
State the fourth principle in your own words: the muqallid mufti gives fatwa based on what ashab al-tarjih have preferred, not weak opinions.
This is a procedural rule, not a substantive fiqh ruling.
2
Understand the role of ashab al-tarjih
Recognize that ashab al-tarjih are mujtahidun fi al-madhhab. They have studied the evidence and may sometimes prefer a companion's view over the Imam's.
Their tarjih is binding, not optional.
3
Recognize the muqallid's limitation
The muqallid mufti is not qualified to weigh evidence himself. He must simply transmit what the rajjih scholars have settled.
This prevents cherry-picking weak opinions.
4
Distinguish from the third principle
The third principle says the default is Imam Abu Hanifa's view. The fourth principle qualifies: if ashab al-tarjih override it based on evidence, that overrides the default.
Both principles work together; the fourth does not contradict the third.
5
Apply to a hypothetical
Suppose ashab al-tarjih have declared that the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view in a particular issue. The muqallid mufti must give fatwa accordingly, even if he personally prefers Imam Abu Hanifa's view.
The muqallid's personal inclination is irrelevant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الرابع: المفتى المقلد يُفتى بما رجّحه أصحاب الترجيح من مشايخ الحنفية، ولا يأخذ بالأقوال المرجوحة.
(۱) العلامة البيري: هو إبراهيم بن حسين بن أحمد بن محمد بن بن أحمد بن بيري (نسبة إلى البيرة وهى موطنه مفتى مكة، ولد فى المدينة المنوّرة سنة ١٠٢٠ هـ وقال في خلاصة الأثر: "كانت ولادته في المدينة المنورة في نيف وعشرين وألف] وتوفّي (رحمه الله) بمكة سنة ١٠٩٩ هـ، ودفن بالمعلاة. كذا على ظهر النسخة المخطوطة لشرح العلامة البيري رحمه الله على الأشباه والنظائر. (۲) شرح عقود رسم المفتى ص ٥٠
لاشك أن الأصل فى مذهب الحنفية أن الفتوى على قول أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى كما فصلناه في الأصل الثالث، ولكن أصحاب الترجيح في المذهب قد يُرجحون مسائل بخلاف ذلك، وذلك لأنهم، كما قال ابن عابدين رحمه الله تعالى: "اطلعوا على دليل الإمام، وعرفوا من أين قال؟ واطلعوا على دليل أصحابه، فقد يُرجحون دليل أصحابه على دليله، فيفتون به. ولا يظن بهم أنهم عدلوا عن قوله لجهلهم بدليله، فإنّا نراهم قد شحَنُوا كتبهم بنصب الأدلة، ثمّ يقولون: الفتوى على قول أبي يوسف مثلاً. وحيث لم نكن نحن أهلاً للنظر في الدليل، ولم نَصِل إلى رتبتهم في حصول شرائط التفريع والتأصيل، فعلينا حكاية ما ينقلونه، لأنهم هم أتباع المذهب الذين نصبوا أنفسهم لتقريره وتحريره باجتهادهم. والحاصل أن أصحاب الترجيح لهم صفتان: الأولى أنهم نصبوا أنفسهم لتنقيح مذهب الحنفية وتحريره، والثانية أنّهم من أهل الاجتهاد الذين هم مخاطبون لقول الإمام أبي حنيفة وأصحابه رحمهم الله تعالى: "لا يحل لأحد أن يُفتى بقولنا حتى يعلم من أين قلنا؟" وبما أن جميع أقوال أصحاب أبى حنيفة رواية عنه أيضاً، كما سبق تفصيله في الأصل الثالث، فهم يأخذون منها ما يترجح دليله عندهم. فالمسئلة التي رجحها هؤلاء يجب على المفتى المقلّد اتباعها، سواء أكان المرجح قولاً للإمام الأعظم، أم لأحدٍ من أصحابه، فهم يرجحون مذهب الصاحبين تارةً ، ومذهب أحدهما أخرى، بل رجحوا قول زفر رحمه الله تعالى في عشرين مسئلة ذكرها ابن عابدين و نظمها في
The Fourth Principle: The muqallid mufti gives fatwa according to what the scholars of tarjih (preponderance) among the Hanafi mashayikh have preferred, and he does not take weak (marjuh) opinions. (1) Al-Allamah al-Bairi: He is Ibrahim bin Husain bin Ahmad bin Muhammad bin bin Ahmad bin Bairi (attributed to al-Bira, which is his hometown, mufti of Makkah. He was born in Madinah al-Munawwarah in the year 1020 AH...). He died (may Allah have mercy on him) in Makkah in the year 1099 AH and was buried in al-Ma'la. This is from the manuscript copy of al-Allamah al-Bairi's Sharh al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair. (2) Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti, p. 50. There is no doubt that the original principle in the Hanafi madhhab is that the fatwa is on the saying of Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) as we detailed in the third principle. But the scholars of tarjih in the madhhab may prefer issues contrary to that, because they, as Ibn Abidin (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'They have studied the Imam's evidence and know from where he said it, and they studied the evidence of his companions. So they may prefer the evidence of his companions over his evidence and give fatwa accordingly. And it should not be thought that they deviated from his saying due to ignorance of his evidence, for we see them filling their books with the presentation of evidences, and then they say: The fatwa is on the saying of Abu Yusuf for example. And since we are not qualified to examine the evidence, and we have not reached their rank in fulfilling the conditions of tafri' and ta'sil, we must narrate what they transmit, because they are the followers of the madhhab who have appointed themselves to its stabilization and editing through their ijtihad. In summary, the scholars of tarjih have two qualities: first, they appointed themselves to refine and edit the Hanafi madhhab; second, they are people of ijtihad who are addressed by the saying of Imam Abu Hanifa and his companions: 'It is not permissible for anyone to give fatwa on our sayings until he knows from where we said it.' And since all the sayings of the companions of Abu Hanifa are also narrations from him, as detailed in the third principle, they take from them what is stronger in evidence in their view. So the issue which these scholars have preferred must be followed by the muqallid mufti, whether the preference is for the saying of the great Imam or for one of his companions. They sometimes prefer the madhhab of the two companions, sometimes that of one of them, and indeed they have preferred the saying of Zufar (may Allah have mercy on him) in twenty issues which Ibn Abidin mentioned and organized in
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage establishes the fourth principle of Rasm al-Mufti: the muqallid mufti (one who is not a mujtahid) must follow the views that have been given tarjih (preponderance) by the senior scholars of the Hanafi school. The author begins with a biographical note on al-Allamah al-Bairi, a major commentator of al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair. Then he explains that although the default rule is to follow Imam Abu Hanifa, the scholars of tarjih may sometimes prefer the companions' views because they have examined the evidence. They are qualified by their ijtihad fi al-madhhab. The muqallid cannot judge the evidence himself; he must rely on their tarjih. The passage quotes Ibn Abidin's famous defense of the rajjih scholars: they know the evidence and do not abandon the Imam's view lightly. The muqallid's duty is simply to transmit what they have settled on. The two qualities of these scholars are highlighted: they are self-appointed editors of the madhhab, and they are capable of ijtihad. They adhere to the principle that one must know the evidence behind the Imam's rulings. The implication is clear: the muqallid should not pick and choose weak opinions; he must follow the tarjih.

Key Terms
أصحاب الترجيح (ashab al-tarjih): Scholars of the Hanafi madhhab who are qualified to weigh between different opinions and give preponderance to one over another

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why might ashab al-tarjih prefer the view of a companion (e.g., Abu Yusuf) over that of Imam Abu Hanifa?
A) Because the companion's view is easier to implement
B) Because they have studied the evidence and found the companion's evidence stronger
C) Because Imam Abu Hanifa's view is always weak
D) Because they want to create diversity in the madhhab

Key Takeaway
The muqallid mufti must follow the tarjih of the qualified scholars of the madhhab; he is not free to pick any opinion.

Fifth Principle: Rely Only on Authoritative Books
Stating the rule that muftis must use only reliable books

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the authoritative books (al-kutub al-mu'tabara) in the Hanafi madhhab and explain why a book may be non-authoritative due to unknown author status.

Core Idea
A mufti must verify a book's authority; not all widely-circulated books are reliable for fatwa.

This lesson introduces one of the most practical principles in mufti adab: verifying the authority of the books used for fatwa. A mufti cannot rely on any Hanafi book that comes to hand; he must know which books the madhhab's authorities have vetted. The passage provides a list of authoritative works and then explains the first reason a book may be non-authoritative—when the author's scholarly standing is unknown.

Guided Path
1
Define 'authoritative books'
Understand that al-kutub al-mu'tabara are those which the deep scholars of the madhhab have relied upon, trusted, and used for issuing fatwas.
2
Identify the listed authoritative books
Memorize the books mentioned by Ibn Abidin and others, such as al-Durr al-Mukhtar, al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir, Jami' al-Rumuz, etc.
These are primary references for fatwa in the Hanafi school.
3
Recognize the first aspect of non-authoritativeness
Learn that a book may be unreliable if the author's condition is unknown—whether he is a trustworthy jurist or not.
The text gives examples: Khulasat al-Kaydani, Khizanat al-Riwayat, and books of al-Qahistani.
4
Apply the principle to examples
Analyze why these specific books are considered non-authoritative despite their popularity.
The reason is lack of knowledge about the author and presence of weak narrations.
